title="Post-exposure prophylaxis">post-exposure prophylaxis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015Fact_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015Fact-4">[4]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Treatment</th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiretroviral_therapy" title="Antiretroviral therapy">Antiretroviral therapy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015Fact_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015Fact-4">[4]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Prognosis" title="Prognosis">Prognosis</a></th><td class="infobox-data">Near normal life expectancy with treatment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC21015Bas_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC21015Bas-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2012Vac_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN2012Vac-6">[6]</a></sup><br/>11 years life expectancy without treatment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNAIDS2007_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNAIDS2007-7">[7]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Frequency</th><td class="infobox-data">64.4 million – 113 million total cases<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2021_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN2021-8">[8]</a></sup><br/>1.5 million new cases (2021)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2021_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN2021-8">[8]</a></sup><br/>38.4 million living with HIV (2021)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2021_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-UN2021-8">[8]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Deaths</th><td class="infobox-data">40.1 million total deaths<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2021_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-UN2021-8">[8]</a></sup><br/>650,000 (2021)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2021_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-UN2021-8">[8]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Human immunodeficiency virus infection and acquired immunodeficiency syndrome</b> (<b>HIV/AIDS</b>) is a spectrum of conditions caused by <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_immunodeficiency_virus" title="Human immunodeficiency virus">human immunodeficiency virus</a> (HIV),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11396444_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11396444-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Retrovirus" title="Retrovirus">retrovirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Following initial infection an individual may not notice any symptoms, or may experience a brief period of <a href="/wiki/Influenza-like_illness" title="Influenza-like illness">influenza-like illness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015Fact_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015Fact-4">[4]</a></sup> Typically, this is followed by a prolonged incubation period with no symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC21015Bas_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC21015Bas-5">[5]</a></sup> If the infection progresses, it interferes more with the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>, increasing the risk of developing common infections such as <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, as well as other <a href="/wiki/Opportunistic_infection" title="Opportunistic infection">opportunistic infections</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumors" title="Tumors">tumors</a> which are otherwise rare in people who have normal immune function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015Fact_4-10"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015Fact-4">[4]</a></sup> These late symptoms of infection are referred to as acquired immunodeficiency syndrome (AIDS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC21015Bas_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC21015Bas-5">[5]</a></sup> This stage is often also associated with <a href="/wiki/Cachexia" title="Cachexia">unintended weight loss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC21015Bas_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-CDC21015Bas-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>HIV is <a href="#Transmission">spread</a> primarily by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unprotected_sex" title="Unprotected sex">unprotected sex</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Anal_sex" title="Anal sex">anal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vaginal_sex" title="Vaginal sex">vaginal sex</a>), contaminated <a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">blood transfusions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypodermic_needle" title="Hypodermic needle">hypodermic needles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vertically_transmitted_infection" title="Vertically transmitted infection">from mother to child</a> during <a href="/wiki/HIV_and_pregnancy" title="HIV and pregnancy">pregnancy</a>, delivery, or breastfeeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TransmissionM2007_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-TransmissionM2007-13">[13]</a></sup> Some bodily fluids, such as saliva, sweat and tears, do not transmit the virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCtransmission_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDCtransmission-14">[14]</a></sup> Oral sex has little to no risk of transmitting the virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methods of prevention include <a href="/wiki/Safe_sex" title="Safe sex">safe sex</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Needle_exchange_program" title="Needle exchange program">needle exchange programs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treatment_as_prevention" title="Treatment as prevention">treating those who are infected</a>, as well as both <a href="/wiki/Pre-exposure_prophylaxis" title="Pre-exposure prophylaxis">pre-</a> and <a href="/wiki/Post-exposure_prophylaxis" title="Post-exposure prophylaxis">post-exposure prophylaxis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015Fact_4-11"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015Fact-4">[4]</a></sup> Disease in a baby can often be prevented by giving both the mother and child <a href="/wiki/Management_of_HIV/AIDS" title="Management of HIV/AIDS">antiretroviral medication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015Fact_4-12"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015Fact-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Known as the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Patient" title="Berlin Patient">Berlin Patient</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Patient" title="London Patient">London Patient</a>, two individuals have been reported cured of AIDS and the United States' National Institutes of Health (NIH) and the Gates Foundation have pledged $200 million focused on developing a global cure for AIDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> While there is no broadly available cure or <a href="/wiki/HIV_vaccine_development" title="HIV vaccine development">vaccine</a>, antiretroviral treatment can slow the course of the disease and may lead to a near-normal life expectancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC21015Bas_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-CDC21015Bas-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2012Vac_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN2012Vac-6">[6]</a></sup> Treatment is recommended as soon as the diagnosis is made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015Tx_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015Tx-17">[17]</a></sup> Without treatment, the average survival time after infection is 11 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNAIDS2007_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNAIDS2007-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2021, about 38 million people worldwide were living with HIV and 650,000 deaths had occurred in that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2021_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-UN2021-8">[8]</a></sup> An estimated 20.6 million of these live in eastern and southern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> 
Between the time that AIDS was identified (in the early 1980s) and 2021, the disease has caused an estimated 40 million deaths worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> HIV/AIDS is considered a <a href="/wiki/Pandemic#HIV/AIDS" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a>—a disease outbreak which is present over a large area and is actively spreading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kallings_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kallings-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>HIV made the jump from other primates to humans in west-central Africa in the early-to-mid 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orgin2011_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orgin2011-21">[21]</a></sup> AIDS was <a href="/wiki/History_of_HIV/AIDS" title="History of HIV/AIDS">first recognized</a> by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> (CDC) in 1981 and its cause—HIV infection—was identified in the early part of the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>
HIV/AIDS has had a large impact on society, both as an illness and as a source of <a href="/wiki/Discrimination_against_people_with_HIV/AIDS" title="Discrimination against people with HIV/AIDS">discrimination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNAIDS2006Ch4_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNAIDS2006Ch4-23">[23]</a></sup> The disease also has large <a href="/wiki/Economic_impact_of_HIV/AIDS" title="Economic impact of HIV/AIDS">economic impacts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNAIDS2006Ch4_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNAIDS2006Ch4-23">[23]</a></sup> There are many <a href="/wiki/Misconceptions_about_HIV/AIDS" title="Misconceptions about HIV/AIDS">misconceptions about HIV/AIDS</a>, such as the belief that it can be transmitted by casual non-sexual contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The disease has become subject to many <a href="/wiki/Religion_and_HIV/AIDS" title="Religion and HIV/AIDS">controversies involving religion</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_and_HIV/AIDS" title="Catholic Church and HIV/AIDS">Catholic Church's position</a> not to support <a href="/wiki/Condom" title="Condom">condom</a> use as prevention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> It has attracted international medical and political attention as well as large-scale funding since it was identified in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn1-59797-294-0_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn1-59797-294-0-26">[26]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><video class="thumbimage" </div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Signs_and_symptoms_of_HIV/AIDS" title="Signs and symptoms of HIV/AIDS">Signs and symptoms of HIV/AIDS</a></div>
<p>There are three main stages of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_immunodeficiency_virus" title="Human immunodeficiency virus">HIV</a> infection: acute infection, clinical latency, and AIDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIDS2010GOV_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-AIDS2010GOV-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M121_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-M121-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acute_infection">Acute infection</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_acute_HIV_infection.svg"><img alt="A diagram of a human torso labeled with the most common symptoms of an acute HIV infection" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1012" data-file-width="1133" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Symptoms_of_acute_HIV_infection.svg/280px-Symptoms_of_acute_HIV_infection.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Symptoms_of_acute_HIV_infection.svg/420px-Symptoms_of_acute_HIV_infection.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Symptoms_of_acute_HIV_infection.svg/560px-Symptoms_of_acute_HIV_infection.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_acute_HIV_infection.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main symptoms of acute HIV infection</div></div></div>
<p>The initial period following the contraction of HIV is called acute HIV, primary HIV or acute retroviral syndrome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M121_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-M121-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup> Many individuals develop an <a href="/wiki/Influenza-like_illness" title="Influenza-like illness">influenza-like illness</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Infectious_mononucleosis" title="Infectious mononucleosis">mononucleosis-like illness</a> 2–4 weeks after exposure while others have no significant symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup> Symptoms occur in 40–90% of cases and most commonly include <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lymphadenopathy" title="Lymphadenopathy">large tender lymph nodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharyngitis" title="Pharyngitis">throat inflammation</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Rash" title="Rash">rash</a>, headache, tiredness, and/or sores of the mouth and genitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup> The rash, which occurs in 20–50% of cases, presents itself on the trunk and is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maculopapular" title="Maculopapular">maculopapular</a>, classically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup> Some people also develop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opportunistic_infections" title="Opportunistic infections">opportunistic infections</a> at this stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup> Gastrointestinal symptoms, such as vomiting or <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a> may occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup> Neurological symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_neuropathy" title="Peripheral neuropathy">peripheral neuropathy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Guillain%E2%80%93Barr%C3%A9_syndrome" title="Guillain–Barré syndrome">Guillain–Barré syndrome</a> also occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup> The duration of the symptoms varies, but is usually one or two weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Owing to their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonspecific_symptoms#Types" title="Nonspecific symptoms">nonspecific</a> character, these symptoms are not often <a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis#Pattern_recognition" title="Medical diagnosis">recognized</a> as signs of HIV infection. Even cases that do get seen by a family doctor or a hospital are often misdiagnosed as one of the many common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious diseases</a> with overlapping symptoms. Thus, it is recommended that HIV be considered in people presenting with an <a href="/wiki/Fever_of_unknown_origin" title="Fever of unknown origin">unexplained fever</a> who may have risk factors for the infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clinical_latency">Clinical latency</span></h3>
<p>The initial symptoms are followed by a stage called clinical latency, asymptomatic HIV, or chronic HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIDS2010GOV_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-AIDS2010GOV-1">[1]</a></sup> Without treatment, this second stage of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_history_of_disease" title="Natural history of disease">natural history</a> of HIV infection can last from about three years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> to over 20 years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> (on average, about eight years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> While typically there are few or no symptoms at first, near the end of this stage many people experience fever, weight loss, gastrointestinal problems and muscle pains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIDS2010GOV_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-AIDS2010GOV-1">[1]</a></sup> Between 50% and 70% of people also develop <a href="/wiki/Persistent_generalized_lymphadenopathy" title="Persistent generalized lymphadenopathy">persistent generalized lymphadenopathy</a>, characterized by unexplained, non-painful enlargement of more than one group of lymph nodes (other than in the groin) for over three to six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M121_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-M121-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIV-1" title="HIV-1">HIV-1</a> infected individuals have a detectable viral load and in the absence of treatment will eventually progress to AIDS, a small proportion (about 5%) retain high levels of <a href="/wiki/CD4" title="CD4">CD4</a><sup>+</sup> T cells (<a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" title="T helper cell">T helper cells</a>) without <a href="/wiki/Management_of_HIV/AIDS" title="Management of HIV/AIDS">antiretroviral therapy</a> for more than five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-6"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LT2010_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-LT2010-35">[35]</a></sup> These individuals are classified as "HIV controllers" or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-term_nonprogressors" title="Long-term nonprogressors">long-term nonprogressors</a> (LTNP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LT2010_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-LT2010-35">[35]</a></sup> Another group consists of those who maintain a low or undetectable viral load without anti-retroviral treatment, known as "elite controllers" or "elite suppressors". They represent approximately 1 in 300 infected persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2007_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walker2007-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquired_immunodeficiency_syndrome">Acquired immunodeficiency syndrome</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_AIDS.svg"><img alt="A diagram of a human torso labeled with the most common symptoms of AIDS" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="882" decoding="async" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Symptoms_of_AIDS.svg/280px-Symptoms_of_AIDS.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Symptoms_of_AIDS.svg/420px-Symptoms_of_AIDS.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Symptoms_of_AIDS.svg/560px-Symptoms_of_AIDS.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_AIDS.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main symptoms of AIDS.</div></div></div>
<p>Acquired immunodeficiency syndrome (AIDS) is defined as an HIV infection with either a CD4<sup>+</sup> T cell count below 200 cells per µL or the occurrence of specific diseases associated with HIV infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-7"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup> In the absence of specific treatment, around half of people infected with HIV develop AIDS within ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-8"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup> The most common initial conditions that alert to the presence of AIDS are <a href="/wiki/Pneumocystis_pneumonia" title="Pneumocystis pneumonia">pneumocystis pneumonia</a> (40%), <a href="/wiki/Cachexia" title="Cachexia">cachexia</a> in the form of HIV wasting syndrome (20%), and <a href="/wiki/Esophageal_candidiasis" title="Esophageal candidiasis">esophageal candidiasis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-9"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup> Other common signs include recurrent <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract_infection" title="Respiratory tract infection">respiratory tract infections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-10"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opportunistic_infections" title="Opportunistic infections">Opportunistic infections</a> may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a> that are normally controlled by the immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes-37">[37]</a></sup> Which infections occur depends partly on what organisms are common in the person's environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-11"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup> These infections may affect nearly every <a href="/wiki/Biological_system" title="Biological system">organ system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Complications2011_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Complications2011-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>People with AIDS have an increased risk of developing various viral-induced cancers, including <a href="/wiki/Kaposi%27s_sarcoma" title="Kaposi's sarcoma">Kaposi's sarcoma</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burkitt%27s_lymphoma" title="Burkitt's lymphoma">Burkitt's lymphoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Primary_central_nervous_system_lymphoma" title="Primary central nervous system lymphoma">primary central nervous system lymphoma</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cervical_cancer" title="Cervical cancer">cervical cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup> Kaposi's sarcoma is the most common cancer, occurring in 10% to 20% of people with HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M169_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-M169-39">[39]</a></sup> The second-most common cancer is lymphoma, which is the cause of death of nearly 16% of people with AIDS and is the initial sign of AIDS in 3% to 4%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M169_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-M169-39">[39]</a></sup> Both these cancers are associated with <a href="/wiki/Kaposi%27s_sarcoma-associated_herpesvirus" title="Kaposi's sarcoma-associated herpesvirus">human herpesvirus 8</a> (HHV-8).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M169_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-M169-39">[39]</a></sup> Cervical cancer occurs more frequently in those with AIDS because of its association with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_papillomavirus" title="Human papillomavirus">human papillomavirus</a> (HPV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M169_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-M169-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Conjunctiva" title="Conjunctiva">Conjunctival cancer</a> (of the layer that lines the inner part of eyelids and the white part of the eye) is also more common in those with HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, people with AIDS frequently have systemic symptoms such as prolonged fevers, <a href="/wiki/Night_sweats" title="Night sweats">sweats</a> (particularly at night), swollen lymph nodes, chills, weakness, and <a href="/wiki/Cachexia" title="Cachexia">unintended weight loss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Diarrhea is another common symptom, present in about 90% of people with AIDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> They can also be affected by diverse psychiatric and neurological symptoms independent of opportunistic infections and cancers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span></h2>

</th></tr></tbody></table>
<p>HIV is spread by three main routes: <a href="/wiki/Human_sexual_activity" title="Human sexual activity">sexual contact</a>, significant exposure to infected body fluids or tissues, and from mother to child during pregnancy, delivery, or breastfeeding (known as <a href="/wiki/Vertical_transmission" title="Vertical transmission">vertical transmission</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TransmissionM2007_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-TransmissionM2007-13">[13]</a></sup> There is no risk of acquiring HIV if exposed to <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a>, nasal secretions, saliva, <a href="/wiki/Sputum" title="Sputum">sputum</a>, sweat, tears, urine, or vomit unless these are contaminated with blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP2007k_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-AFP2007k-52">[52]</a></sup> It is also possible to be <a href="/wiki/Coinfection" title="Coinfection">co-infected</a> by more than one strain of HIV—a condition known as <a href="/wiki/HIV_superinfection" title="HIV superinfection">HIV superinfection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual">Sexual</span></h3>
<p>The most frequent mode of transmission of HIV is through sexual contact with an infected person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TransmissionM2007_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-TransmissionM2007-13">[13]</a></sup> However, an HIV-positive person who has an undetectable viral load as a result of long-term treatment has effectively no risk of transmitting HIV sexually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCUndetectable_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDCUndetectable-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The existence of functionally noncontagious HIV-positive people on antiretroviral therapy was controversially publicized in the 2008 <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Statement" title="Swiss Statement">Swiss Statement</a>, and has since become accepted as medically sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Globally, the most common mode of HIV transmission is via <a href="/wiki/Heterosexuality" title="Heterosexuality">sexual contacts between people of the opposite sex</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TransmissionM2007_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-TransmissionM2007-13">[13]</a></sup> however, the pattern of transmission varies among countries. As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HIV/AIDS&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, most HIV transmission in the United States occurred among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Men_who_had_sex_with_men" title="Men who had sex with men">men who had sex with men</a> (82% of new HIV diagnoses among males aged 13 and older and 70% of total new diagnoses).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In the US, gay and bisexual men aged 13 to 24 accounted for an estimated 92% of new HIV diagnoses among all men in their age group and 27% of new diagnoses among all gay and bisexual men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2016Bi_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2016Bi-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>With regard to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unprotected_sex" title="Unprotected sex">unprotected</a> heterosexual contacts, estimates of the risk of HIV transmission per sexual act appear to be four to ten times higher in low-income countries than in high-income countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boily2009_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boily2009-60">[60]</a></sup> In low-income countries, the risk of female-to-male transmission is estimated as 0.38% per act, and of male-to-female transmission as 0.30% per act; the equivalent estimates for high-income countries are 0.04% per act for female-to-male transmission, and 0.08% per act for male-to-female transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boily2009_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boily2009-60">[60]</a></sup> The risk of transmission from anal intercourse is especially high, estimated as 1.4–1.7% per act in both heterosexual and homosexual contacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boily2009_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Boily2009-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> While the risk of transmission from <a href="/wiki/Oral_sex" title="Oral sex">oral sex</a> is relatively low, it is still present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The risk from receiving oral sex has been described as "nearly nil";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> however, a few cases have been reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The per-act risk is estimated at 0–0.04% for receptive oral intercourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dosekun2010_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dosekun2010-65">[65]</a></sup> In settings involving <a href="/wiki/Prostitution" title="Prostitution">prostitution</a> in low-income countries, risk of female-to-male transmission has been estimated as 2.4% per act, and of male-to-female transmission as 0.05% per act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boily2009_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-Boily2009-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Risk of transmission increases in the presence of many <a href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_infection" title="Sexually transmitted infection">sexually transmitted infections</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CochraneSTI2012_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-CochraneSTI2012-66">[66]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Genital_ulcer" title="Genital ulcer">genital ulcers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boily2009_60-4"><a href="#cite_note-Boily2009-60">[60]</a></sup> Genital ulcers appear to increase the risk approximately fivefold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boily2009_60-5"><a href="#cite_note-Boily2009-60">[60]</a></sup> Other sexually transmitted infections, such as <a href="/wiki/Gonorrhea" title="Gonorrhea">gonorrhea</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlamydia_infection" title="Chlamydia infection">chlamydia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trichomoniasis" title="Trichomoniasis">trichomoniasis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_vaginosis" title="Bacterial vaginosis">bacterial vaginosis</a>, are associated with somewhat smaller increases in risk of transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dosekun2010_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dosekun2010-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Viral_load" title="Viral load">viral load</a> of an infected person is an important risk factor in both sexual and mother-to-child transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> During the first 2.5 months of an HIV infection a person's infectiousness is twelve times higher due to the high viral load associated with acute HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dosekun2010_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dosekun2010-65">[65]</a></sup> If the person is in the late stages of infection, rates of transmission are approximately eightfold greater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boily2009_60-6"><a href="#cite_note-Boily2009-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercial sex workers (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_the_pornographic_film_industry" title="HIV/AIDS in the pornographic film industry">those in pornography</a>) have an increased likelihood of contracting HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Rough sex can be a factor associated with an increased risk of transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sexual_assault" title="Sexual assault">Sexual assault</a> is also believed to carry an increased risk of HIV transmission as condoms are rarely worn, physical trauma to the vagina or rectum is likely, and there may be a greater risk of concurrent sexually transmitted infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Body_fluids">Body fluids</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AIDS_Poster_If_You%27re_Dabbling_in_Drugs_1989.jpg"><img alt='A black-and-white poster of a young black man with a towel in his left hand with the words "If you are dabbling with drugs you could be dabbling with your life" above him' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1269" data-file-width="937" decoding="async" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/AIDS_Poster_If_You%27re_Dabbling_in_Drugs_1989.jpg/220px-AIDS_Poster_If_You%27re_Dabbling_in_Drugs_1989.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/AIDS_Poster_If_You%27re_Dabbling_in_Drugs_1989.jpg/330px-AIDS_Poster_If_You%27re_Dabbling_in_Drugs_1989.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/AIDS_Poster_If_You%27re_Dabbling_in_Drugs_1989.jpg/440px-AIDS_Poster_If_You%27re_Dabbling_in_Drugs_1989.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AIDS_Poster_If_You%27re_Dabbling_in_Drugs_1989.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>CDC poster from 1989 highlighting the threat of AIDS associated with drug use</div></div></div>
<p>The second-most frequent mode of HIV transmission is via blood and blood products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TransmissionM2007_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-TransmissionM2007-13">[13]</a></sup> Blood-borne transmission can be through needle-sharing during intravenous drug use, needle-stick injury, transfusion of contaminated blood or blood product, or medical injections with unsterilized equipment. The risk from sharing a needle during <a href="/wiki/Drug_injection" title="Drug injection">drug injection</a> is between 0.63% and 2.4% per act, with an average of 0.8%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Risk2006_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Risk2006-72">[72]</a></sup> The risk of acquiring HIV from a needle stick from an HIV-infected person is estimated as 0.3% (about 1 in 333) per act and the risk following <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucous membrane</a> exposure to infected blood as 0.09% (about 1 in 1000) per act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP2007k_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-AFP2007k-52">[52]</a></sup> This risk may, however, be up to 5% if the introduced blood was from a person with a high viral load and the cut was deep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Needle2002_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Needle2002-73">[73]</a></sup> In the United States, intravenous drug users made up 12% of all new cases of HIV in 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TransmissionCDC2012_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-TransmissionCDC2012-74">[74]</a></sup> and in some areas more than 80% of people who inject drugs are HIV-positive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TransmissionM2007_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-TransmissionM2007-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>HIV is transmitted in about 90% of <a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">blood transfusions</a> using infected blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blood_Transfusion_Risk_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blood_Transfusion_Risk-44">[44]</a></sup> In developed countries the risk of acquiring HIV from a blood transfusion is extremely low (less than one in half a million) where improved donor selection and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIV_screening" title="HIV screening">HIV screening</a> is performed;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TransmissionM2007_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-TransmissionM2007-13">[13]</a></sup> for example, in the UK the risk is reported at one in five million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and in the United States it was one in 1.5 million in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In low-income countries, only half of transfusions may be appropriately screened (as of 2008),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2011Seventy_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN2011Seventy-77">[77]</a></sup> and it is estimated that up to 15% of HIV infections in these areas come from transfusion of infected blood and blood products, representing between 5% and 10% of global infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TransmissionM2007_13-7"><a href="#cite_note-TransmissionM2007-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO070401_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO070401-78">[78]</a></sup> It is possible to acquire HIV from organ and tissue <a href="/wiki/Organ_transplantation" title="Organ transplantation">transplantation</a>, although this is rare because of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIV_screening" title="HIV screening">screening</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unsafe medical injections play a role in <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_Africa" title="HIV/AIDS in Africa">HIV spread in sub-Saharan Africa</a>. In 2007, between 12% and 17% of infections in this region were attributed to medical syringe use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UnsafeInjection2009_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-UnsafeInjection2009-80">[80]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> estimates the risk of transmission as a result of a medical injection in Africa at 1.2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UnsafeInjection2009_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-UnsafeInjection2009-80">[80]</a></sup> Risks are also associated with invasive procedures, assisted delivery, and dental care in this area of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UnsafeInjection2009_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-UnsafeInjection2009-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>People giving or receiving <a href="/wiki/Tattoo" title="Tattoo">tattoos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Body_piercing" title="Body piercing">piercings</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scarification" title="Scarification">scarification</a> are theoretically at risk of infection but no confirmed cases have been documented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCBasics2012_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDCBasics2012-81">[81]</a></sup> It is not possible for <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquitoes</a> or other insects to transmit HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C4Wauto-8503951_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-C4Wauto-8503951-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mother-to-child">Mother-to-child</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/HIV_and_pregnancy" title="HIV and pregnancy">HIV and pregnancy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIV_and_breastfeeding" title="HIV and breastfeeding">HIV and breastfeeding</a></div>
<p>HIV can be transmitted from mother to child during pregnancy, during delivery, or through breast milk, resulting in the baby also contracting HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TransmissionM2007_13-8"><a href="#cite_note-TransmissionM2007-13">[13]</a></sup> As of 2008, vertical transmission accounted for about 90% of cases of HIV in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mother2010_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mother2010-84">[84]</a></sup> In the absence of treatment, the risk of transmission before or during birth is around 20%, and in those who also breastfeed 35%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mother2010_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mother2010-84">[84]</a></sup> Treatment decreases this risk to less than 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antiretrovirals when taken by either the mother or the baby decrease the risk of transmission in those who do breastfeed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> If blood contaminates food during <a href="/wiki/Premastication" title="Premastication">pre-chewing</a> it may pose a risk of transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCBasics2012_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDCBasics2012-81">[81]</a></sup> If a woman is untreated, two years of breastfeeding results in an HIV/AIDS risk in her baby of about 17%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011Breast_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011Breast-87">[87]</a></sup> Due to the increased risk of death without breastfeeding in many areas in the developing world, the World Health Organization recommends either exclusive breastfeeding or the provision of safe formula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011Breast_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011Breast-87">[87]</a></sup> All women known to be HIV-positive should be taking lifelong antiretroviral therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011Breast_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011Breast-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Virology">Virology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HI-virion-structure_en.svg"><img alt="diagram of microscopic viron structure" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/HI-virion-structure_en.svg/220px-HI-virion-structure_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/HI-virion-structure_en.svg/330px-HI-virion-structure_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/HI-virion-structure_en.svg/440px-HI-virion-structure_en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HI-virion-structure_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of a HIV virion structure</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HIV-budding-Color.jpg"><img alt="A large round blue object with a smaller red object attached to it. Multiple small green spots are speckled over both." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1971" data-file-width="2967" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HIV-budding-Color.jpg/220px-HIV-budding-Color.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HIV-budding-Color.jpg/330px-HIV-budding-Color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/HIV-budding-Color.jpg/440px-HIV-budding-Color.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HIV-budding-Color.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_micrograph" title="Scanning electron micrograph">Scanning electron micrograph</a> of HIV-1, colored green, budding from a cultured <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocyte</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> is the cause of the spectrum of disease known as HIV/AIDS. HIV is a <a href="/wiki/Retrovirus" title="Retrovirus">retrovirus</a> that primarily infects components of the human <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> such as CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells, <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_cell" title="Dendritic cell">dendritic cells</a>. It directly and indirectly destroys CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alimonti_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alimonti-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>HIV is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Lentivirus" title="Lentivirus">Lentivirus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICTV61.0.6_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICTV61.0.6-89">[89]</a></sup> part of the family <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retroviridae" title="Retroviridae">Retroviridae</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICTV61._90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICTV61.-90">[90]</a></sup> Lentiviruses share many <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological</a> characteristics. Many species of mammals are infected by lentiviruses, which are characteristically responsible for long-duration illnesses with a long <a href="/wiki/Incubation_period" title="Incubation period">incubation period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levy-91">[91]</a></sup> Lentiviruses are transmitted as single-stranded, positive-<a href="/wiki/Sense_(molecular_biology)" title="Sense (molecular biology)">sense</a>, enveloped <a href="/wiki/RNA_virus" title="RNA virus">RNA viruses</a>. Upon entry into the target cell, the viral <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> is converted (reverse transcribed) into double-stranded <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> by a virally encoded <a href="/wiki/Reverse_transcriptase" title="Reverse transcriptase">reverse transcriptase</a> that is transported along with the viral genome in the virus particle. The resulting viral DNA is then imported into the cell nucleus and integrated into the cellular DNA by a virally encoded <a href="/wiki/Integrase" title="Integrase">integrase</a> and host co-factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JASmith_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-JASmith-92">[92]</a></sup> Once integrated, the virus may become <a href="/wiki/Incubation_period" title="Incubation period">latent</a>, allowing the virus and its host cell to avoid detection by the immune system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Alternatively, the virus may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcribed</a>, producing new RNA genomes and viral proteins that are packaged and released from the cell as new virus particles that begin the replication cycle anew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>HIV is now known to spread between CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells by two parallel routes: cell-free spread and cell-to-cell spread, i.e. it employs hybrid spreading mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang-95">[95]</a></sup> In the cell-free spread, virus particles bud from an infected T cell, enter the blood/extracellular fluid and then infect another T cell following a chance encounter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang-95">[95]</a></sup> HIV can also disseminate by direct transmission from one cell to another by a process of cell-to-cell spread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jolly_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jolly-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sattentau_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sattentau-97">[97]</a></sup> The hybrid spreading mechanisms of HIV contribute to the virus's ongoing replication against antiretroviral therapies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sigal_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sigal-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two <a href="/wiki/Subtypes_of_HIV" title="Subtypes of HIV">types of HIV</a> have been characterized: HIV-1 and HIV-2. HIV-1 is the virus that was originally discovered (and initially referred to also as LAV or HTLV-III). It is more <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulent</a>, more <a href="/wiki/Infectivity" title="Infectivity">infective</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> and is the cause of the majority of HIV infections globally. The lower infectivity of HIV-2 as compared with HIV-1 implies that fewer people exposed to HIV-2 will be infected per exposure. Because of its relatively poor capacity for transmission, HIV-2 is largely confined to <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reeves_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reeves-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pathophysiology_of_HIV/AIDS" title="Pathophysiology of HIV/AIDS">Pathophysiology of HIV/AIDS</a></div>

codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><track data-dir="ltr" kind="subtitles" label="English ‪(en)‬" src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/api.php?action=timedtext&amp;title=File%3AHIV_and_AIDS_explained_in_a_simple_way.webm&amp;lang=en&amp;trackformat=vtt&amp;origin=%2A" srclang="en" type="text/vtt"/><track data-dir="ltr" kind="subtitles" label="қазақша ‪(kk)‬" src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/api.php?action=timedtext&amp;title=File%3AHIV_and_AIDS_explained_in_a_simple_way.webm&amp;lang=kk&amp;trackformat=vtt&amp;origin=%2A" srclang="kk" type="text/vtt"/><track data-dir="ltr" kind="subtitles" label="Tiếng Việt ‪(vi)‬" src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/api.php?action=timedtext&amp;title=File%3AHIV_and_AIDS_explained_in_a_simple_way.webm&amp;lang=vi&amp;trackformat=vtt&amp;origin=%2A" srclang="vi" type="text/vtt"/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HIV_and_AIDS_explained_in_a_simple_way.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HIV/AIDS explained in a simple way</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hiv_replication_cycle.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="545" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Hiv_replication_cycle.gif/310px-Hiv_replication_cycle.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Hiv_replication_cycle.gif/465px-Hiv_replication_cycle.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/99/Hiv_replication_cycle.gif 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hiv_replication_cycle.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HIV replication cycle</div></div></div>
<p>After the virus enters the body, there is a period of rapid <a href="/wiki/Viral_replication" title="Viral replication">viral replication</a>, leading to an abundance of virus in the peripheral blood. During primary infection, the level of HIV may reach several million virus particles per milliliter of blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piatak_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piatak-101">[101]</a></sup> This response is accompanied by a marked drop in the number of circulating <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" title="T helper cell">CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells</a>. The acute <a href="/wiki/Viremia" title="Viremia">viremia</a> is almost invariably associated with activation of <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxic_T_cell" title="Cytotoxic T cell">CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells</a>, which kill HIV-infected cells, and subsequently with antibody production, or <a href="/wiki/Seroconversion" title="Seroconversion">seroconversion</a>. The CD8<sup>+</sup> T cell response is thought to be important in controlling virus levels, which peak and then decline, as the CD4<sup>+</sup> T cell counts recover. A good CD8<sup>+</sup> T cell response has been linked to slower disease progression and a better prognosis, though it does not eliminate the virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pantaleo1998_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pantaleo1998-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ultimately, HIV causes AIDS by depleting CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells. This weakens the immune system and allows <a href="/wiki/Opportunistic_infection" title="Opportunistic infection">opportunistic infections</a>. T cells are essential to the immune response and without them, the body cannot fight infections or kill cancerous cells. The mechanism of CD4<sup>+</sup> T cell depletion differs in the acute and chronic phases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16679064_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16679064-103">[103]</a></sup> During the acute phase, HIV-induced cell lysis and killing of infected cells by CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells accounts for CD4<sup>+</sup> T cell depletion, although <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a> may also be a factor. During the chronic phase, the consequences of generalized immune activation coupled with the gradual loss of the ability of the immune system to generate new T cells appear to account for the slow decline in CD4<sup>+</sup> T cell numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the symptoms of immune deficiency characteristic of AIDS do not appear for years after a person is infected, the bulk of CD4<sup>+</sup> T cell loss occurs during the first weeks of infection, especially in the intestinal mucosa, which harbors the majority of the lymphocytes found in the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15365095_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15365095-105">[105]</a></sup> The reason for the preferential loss of mucosal CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells is that the majority of mucosal CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells express the <a href="/wiki/CCR5" title="CCR5">CCR5</a> protein which HIV uses as a <a href="/wiki/Co-receptor" title="Co-receptor">co-receptor</a> to gain access to the cells, whereas only a small fraction of CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells in the bloodstream do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15365096_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15365096-106">[106]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CCR5-%CE%9432" title="CCR5-Δ32">specific genetic change</a> that alters the CCR5 protein when present in both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromosomes" title="Chromosomes">chromosomes</a> very effectively prevents HIV-1 infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>HIV seeks out and destroys CCR5 expressing CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells during acute infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Julio2011_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Julio2011-108">[108]</a></sup> A vigorous immune response eventually controls the infection and initiates the clinically latent phase. CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells in mucosal tissues remain particularly affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Julio2011_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Julio2011-108">[108]</a></sup> Continuous HIV replication causes a state of generalized immune activation persisting throughout the chronic phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18161758_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18161758-109">[109]</a></sup> Immune activation, which is reflected by the increased activation state of immune cells and release of pro-inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a>, results from the activity of several HIV <a href="/wiki/Gene_product" title="Gene product">gene products</a> and the immune response to ongoing HIV replication. It is also linked to the breakdown of the immune surveillance system of the gastrointestinal mucosal barrier caused by the depletion of mucosal CD4<sup>+</sup> T cells during the acute phase of disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17115046_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17115046-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diagnosis_of_HIV/AIDS" title="Diagnosis of HIV/AIDS">Diagnosis of HIV/AIDS</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hiv-timecourse.png"><img alt="A graph with two lines. One in blue moves from high on the right to low on the left with a brief rise in the middle. The second line in red moves from zero to very high then drops to low and gradually rises to high again" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="414" data-file-width="738" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Hiv-timecourse.png/300px-Hiv-timecourse.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Hiv-timecourse.png/450px-Hiv-timecourse.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Hiv-timecourse.png/600px-Hiv-timecourse.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hiv-timecourse.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A generalized graph of the relationship between HIV copies (viral load) and CD4<sup>+</sup> T cell counts over the average course of untreated HIV infection. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-line mw-no-invert" style="display: inline-block; vertical-align: middle; width: 1.67em; height: 0; border-style: none; border-top: 2px dotted black;border-top:blue solid 2px;"> </span> CD4<sup>+</sup> T Lymphocyte count (cells/mm³)</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-line mw-no-invert" style="display: inline-block; vertical-align: middle; width: 1.67em; height: 0; border-style: none; border-top: 2px dotted black;border-top:red solid 2px;"> </span> HIV RNA copies per mL of plasma</div></div></div></div>
