<p>HIV/AIDS is diagnosed via laboratory testing and then staged based on the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS_defining_clinical_condition" title="AIDS defining clinical condition">certain signs or symptoms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup> HIV screening is recommended by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Preventive_Services_Task_Force" title="United States Preventive Services Task Force">United States Preventive Services Task Force</a> for all people 15 years to 65 years of age, including all pregnant women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USP2019Screen_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-USP2019Screen-112">[112]</a></sup> Additionally, testing is recommended for those at high risk, which includes anyone diagnosed with a sexually transmitted illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USP2019Screen_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-USP2019Screen-112">[112]</a></sup> In many areas of the world, a third of HIV carriers only discover they are infected at an advanced stage of the disease when AIDS or severe immunodeficiency has become apparent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="HIV_testing">HIV testing</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HIV_Rapid_Test_being_administered.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/HIV_Rapid_Test_being_administered.jpg/220px-HIV_Rapid_Test_being_administered.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/HIV_Rapid_Test_being_administered.jpg/330px-HIV_Rapid_Test_being_administered.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/HIV_Rapid_Test_being_administered.jpg/440px-HIV_Rapid_Test_being_administered.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HIV_Rapid_Test_being_administered.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HIV Rapid Test being administered</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oraquick.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2091" data-file-width="1638" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Oraquick.jpg/220px-Oraquick.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Oraquick.jpg/330px-Oraquick.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Oraquick.jpg/440px-Oraquick.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oraquick.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oraquick</div></div></div>
<p>Most people infected with HIV develop specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Seroconversion" title="Seroconversion">seroconvert</a>) within three to twelve weeks after the initial infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-12"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup> Diagnosis of primary HIV before seroconversion is done by measuring HIV-<a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P24_antigen#Antigen_tests" title="P24 antigen">p24 antigen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-13"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup> Positive results obtained by antibody or <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">PCR</a> testing are confirmed either by a different antibody or by PCR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antibody tests in children younger than 18 months are typically inaccurate, due to the continued presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maternal_Passive_Immunity#Naturally_acquired_passive_immunity" title="Maternal Passive Immunity">maternal antibodies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChildDiag2010_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChildDiag2010-113">[113]</a></sup> Thus HIV infection can only be diagnosed by PCR testing for HIV RNA or DNA, or via testing for the p24 antigen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup> Much of the world lacks access to reliable PCR testing, and people in many places simply wait until either symptoms develop or the child is old enough for accurate antibody testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChildDiag2010_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChildDiag2010-113">[113]</a></sup> In sub-Saharan Africa between 2007 and 2009, between 30% and 70% of the population were aware of their HIV status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2011Eighty_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN2011Eighty-114">[114]</a></sup> In 2009, between 3.6% and 42% of men and women in sub-Saharan countries were tested;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2011Eighty_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN2011Eighty-114">[114]</a></sup> this represented a significant increase compared to previous years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2011Eighty_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-UN2011Eighty-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classifications">Classifications</span></h3>
<p>Two main clinical staging systems are used to classify HIV and HIV-related disease for <a href="/wiki/Disease_surveillance" title="Disease surveillance">surveillance</a> purposes: the <a href="/wiki/WHO_disease_staging_system_for_HIV_infection_and_disease" title="WHO disease staging system for HIV infection and disease">WHO disease staging system for HIV infection and disease</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/CDC_classification_system_for_HIV_infection" title="CDC classification system for HIV infection">CDC classification system for HIV infection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCCase2008_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDCCase2008-115">[115]</a></sup> The CDC's classification system is more frequently adopted in developed countries. Since the WHO's staging system does not require laboratory tests, it is suited to the resource-restricted conditions encountered in developing countries, where it can also be used to help guide clinical management. Despite their differences, the two systems allow a comparison for statistical purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M121_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-M121-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCCase2008_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDCCase2008-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>The World Health Organization first proposed a definition for AIDS in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-8"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup> Since then, the WHO classification has been updated and expanded several times, with the most recent version being published in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-9"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup> The WHO system uses the following categories:
</p>
<ul><li>Primary HIV infection: May be either asymptomatic or associated with acute retroviral syndrome<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-10"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li>Stage I: HIV infection is <a href="/wiki/Asymptomatic" title="Asymptomatic">asymptomatic</a> with a CD4<sup>+</sup> T cell count (also known as CD4 count) greater than 500 per microlitre (µl or cubic mm) of blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-11"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup> May include generalized lymph node enlargement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-12"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li>Stage II: Mild symptoms, which may include minor <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucocutaneous</a> manifestations and recurrent <a href="/wiki/Upper_respiratory_tract_infection" title="Upper respiratory tract infection">upper respiratory tract infections</a>. A CD4 count of less than 500/µl<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-13"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li>Stage III: Advanced symptoms, which may include unexplained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronic_(medical)" title="Chronic (medical)">chronic</a> diarrhea for longer than a month, severe bacterial infections including tuberculosis of the lung, and a CD4 count of less than 350/µl<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-14"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li>Stage IV or AIDS: severe symptoms, which include <a href="/wiki/Toxoplasmosis" title="Toxoplasmosis">toxoplasmosis</a> of the brain, <a href="/wiki/Candidiasis" title="Candidiasis">candidiasis</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrate_trachea" title="Vertebrate trachea">trachea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchus" title="Bronchus">bronchi</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kaposi%27s_sarcoma" title="Kaposi's sarcoma">Kaposi's sarcoma</a>. A CD4 count of less than 200/µl<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCase2007_28-15"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCase2007-28">[28]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The U.S. Centers for Disease Control and Prevention also created a classification system for HIV, and updated it in 2008 and 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDCCase2008_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDCCase2008-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2014Clas_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2014Clas-116">[116]</a></sup> This system classifies HIV infections based on CD4 count and clinical symptoms, and describes the infection in five groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2014Clas_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2014Clas-116">[116]</a></sup> In those greater than six years of age it is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2014Clas_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2014Clas-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Stage 0: the time between a negative or indeterminate HIV test followed less than 180 days by a positive test.</li>
<li>Stage 1: CD4 count ≥ 500 cells/µl and no AIDS-defining conditions.</li>
<li>Stage 2: CD4 count 200 to 500 cells/µl and no AIDS-defining conditions.</li>
<li>Stage 3: CD4 count ≤ 200 cells/µl or AIDS-defining conditions.</li>
<li>Unknown: if insufficient information is available to make any of the above classifications.</li></ul>
<p>For surveillance purposes, the AIDS diagnosis still stands even if, after treatment, the CD4<sup>+</sup> T cell count rises to above 200 per µL of blood or other AIDS-defining illnesses are cured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M121_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-M121-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prevention_of_HIV/AIDS" title="Prevention of HIV/AIDS">Prevention of HIV/AIDS</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AIDS_Clinic,_McLeod_Ganj,_2010.jpg"><img alt="A run down a two-story building with several signs related to AIDS prevention" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="940" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/AIDS_Clinic%2C_McLeod_Ganj%2C_2010.jpg/220px-AIDS_Clinic%2C_McLeod_Ganj%2C_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/AIDS_Clinic%2C_McLeod_Ganj%2C_2010.jpg/330px-AIDS_Clinic%2C_McLeod_Ganj%2C_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/AIDS_Clinic%2C_McLeod_Ganj%2C_2010.jpg/440px-AIDS_Clinic%2C_McLeod_Ganj%2C_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AIDS_Clinic,_McLeod_Ganj,_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>AIDS Clinic, <a href="/wiki/McLeod_Ganj" title="McLeod Ganj">McLeod Ganj</a>, Himachal Pradesh, India, 2010</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_contact">Sexual contact</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FACING_AIDS_a_condom_and_a_pill_at_a_time_-_I_am_FACING_AIDS_because_people_I_-3_are_infected._(5202985364).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="855" data-file-width="1161" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/FACING_AIDS_a_condom_and_a_pill_at_a_time_-_I_am_FACING_AIDS_because_people_I_-3_are_infected._%285202985364%29.jpg/220px-FACING_AIDS_a_condom_and_a_pill_at_a_time_-_I_am_FACING_AIDS_because_people_I_-3_are_infected._%285202985364%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/FACING_AIDS_a_condom_and_a_pill_at_a_time_-_I_am_FACING_AIDS_because_people_I_-3_are_infected._%285202985364%29.jpg/330px-FACING_AIDS_a_condom_and_a_pill_at_a_time_-_I_am_FACING_AIDS_because_people_I_-3_are_infected._%285202985364%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/FACING_AIDS_a_condom_and_a_pill_at_a_time_-_I_am_FACING_AIDS_because_people_I_-3_are_infected._%285202985364%29.jpg/440px-FACING_AIDS_a_condom_and_a_pill_at_a_time_-_I_am_FACING_AIDS_because_people_I_-3_are_infected._%285202985364%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FACING_AIDS_a_condom_and_a_pill_at_a_time_-_I_am_FACING_AIDS_because_people_I_-3_are_infected._(5202985364).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>People wearing AIDS awareness signs. on the left: "Facing AIDS a condom and a pill at a time"; on the right: "I am Facing AIDS because people I ♥ are infected"</div></div></div>
<p>Consistent <a href="/wiki/Condom" title="Condom">condom</a> use reduces the risk of HIV transmission by approximately 80% over the long term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> When condoms are used consistently by a couple in which one person is infected, the rate of HIV infection is less than 1% per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCondoms_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCondoms-118">[118]</a></sup> There is some evidence to suggest that <a href="/wiki/Female_condom" title="Female condom">female condoms</a> may provide an equivalent level of protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Application of a vaginal gel containing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenofovir" title="Tenofovir">tenofovir</a> (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverse_transcriptase_inhibitor" title="Reverse transcriptase inhibitor">reverse transcriptase inhibitor</a>) immediately before sex seems to reduce infection rates by approximately 40% among African women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VagGel2012_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-VagGel2012-120">[120]</a></sup> By contrast, use of the <a href="/wiki/Spermicide" title="Spermicide">spermicide</a> <a href="/wiki/Nonoxynol-9" title="Nonoxynol-9">nonoxynol-9</a> may increase the risk of transmission due to its tendency to cause vaginal and rectal irritation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">Circumcision</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> "reduces the acquisition of HIV by heterosexual men by between 38% and 66% over 24 months".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Owing to these studies, both the World Health Organization and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNAIDS" title="UNAIDS">UNAIDS</a> recommended male circumcision in 2007 as a method of preventing female-to-male HIV transmission in areas with high rates of HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> However, whether it protects against male-to-female transmission is disputed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19849961_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19849961-125">[125]</a></sup> and whether it is of benefit in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_countries" title="Developed countries">developed countries</a> and among <a href="/wiki/Men_who_have_sex_with_men" title="Men who have sex with men">men who have sex with men</a> is undetermined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>Programs encouraging <a href="/wiki/Abstinence-only_sex_education" title="Abstinence-only sex education">sexual abstinence</a> do not appear to affect subsequent HIV risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Evidence of any benefit from <a href="/wiki/Peer_education" title="Peer education">peer education</a> is equally poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22641791_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22641791-130">[130]</a></sup> Comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">sexual education</a> provided at school may decrease high-risk behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> A substantial minority of young people continues to engage in high-risk practices despite knowing about HIV/AIDS, underestimating their own risk of becoming infected with HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patel2008_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patel2008-133">[133]</a></sup> Voluntary counseling and testing people for HIV does not affect risky behavior in those who test negative but does increase condom use in those who test positive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Enhanced family planning services appear to increase the likelihood of women with HIV using contraception, compared to basic services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> It is not known whether treating other sexually transmitted infections is effective in preventing HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CochraneSTI2012_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-CochraneSTI2012-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-exposure">Pre-exposure</span></h3>
<p>Antiretroviral treatment among people with HIV whose CD4 count ≤ 550 cells/µL is a very effective way to prevent HIV infection of their partner (a strategy known as treatment as prevention, or TASP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anglemyer2013_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anglemyer2013-136">[136]</a></sup> TASP is associated with a 10- to 20-fold reduction in transmission risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anglemyer2013_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anglemyer2013-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chou2012_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chou2012-137">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pre-exposure_prophylaxis" title="Pre-exposure prophylaxis">Pre-exposure prophylaxis</a> (PrEP) with a daily dose of the medications <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenofovir" title="Tenofovir">tenofovir</a>, with or without <a href="/wiki/Emtricitabine" title="Emtricitabine">emtricitabine</a>, is effective in people at high risk including men who have sex with men, couples where one is HIV-positive, and young heterosexuals in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VagGel2012_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-VagGel2012-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> It may also be effective in intravenous drug users, with a study finding a decrease in risk of 0.7 to 0.4 per 100 person years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USPSTF" title="USPSTF">USPSTF</a>, in 2019, recommended PrEP in those who are at high risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Universal_precautions" title="Universal precautions">Universal precautions</a> within the health care environment are believed to be effective in decreasing the risk of HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intravenous_drug_use" title="Intravenous drug use">Intravenous drug use</a> is an important risk factor, and <a href="/wiki/Harm_reduction" title="Harm reduction">harm reduction</a> strategies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Needle-exchange_program" title="Needle-exchange program">needle-exchange programs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opioid_replacement_therapy" title="Opioid replacement therapy">opioid substitution therapy</a> appear effective in decreasing this risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kurth2011_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kurth2011-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-exposure">Post-exposure</span></h3>
<p>A course of antiretrovirals administered within 48 to 72 hours after exposure to HIV-positive blood or genital secretions is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Post-exposure_prophylaxis" title="Post-exposure prophylaxis">post-exposure prophylaxis</a> (PEP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prevention2012_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prevention2012-144">[144]</a></sup> The use of the single agent <a href="/wiki/Zidovudine" title="Zidovudine">zidovudine</a> reduces the risk of a HIV infection five-fold following a needle-stick injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prevention2012_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prevention2012-144">[144]</a></sup> As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HIV/AIDS&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the prevention regimen recommended in the United States consists of three medications—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenofovir" title="Tenofovir">tenofovir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emtricitabine" title="Emtricitabine">emtricitabine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raltegravir" title="Raltegravir">raltegravir</a>—as this may reduce the risk further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>PEP treatment is recommended after a <a href="/wiki/Sexual_assault" title="Sexual assault">sexual assault</a> when the perpetrator is known to be HIV-positive, but is controversial when their HIV status is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2011Sex_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM2011Sex-146">[146]</a></sup> The duration of treatment is usually four weeks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CochranePEP2007_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-CochranePEP2007-147">[147]</a></sup> and is frequently associated with adverse effects—where zidovudine is used, about 70% of cases result in adverse effects such as nausea (24%), fatigue (22%), emotional distress (13%) and headaches (9%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP2007k_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-AFP2007k-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mother-to-child_2">Mother-to-child</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/HIV_and_pregnancy" title="HIV and pregnancy">HIV and pregnancy</a></div>
<p>Programs to prevent the <a href="/wiki/Vertical_transmission" title="Vertical transmission">vertical transmission</a> of HIV (from mothers to children) can reduce rates of transmission by 92–99%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mother2010_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mother2010-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kurth2011_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kurth2011-142">[142]</a></sup> This primarily involves the use of a combination of antiviral medications during pregnancy and after birth in the infant, and potentially includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bottle_feeding" title="Bottle feeding">bottle feeding</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfeeding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mother2010_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mother2010-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> If replacement feeding is acceptable, feasible, affordable, sustainable and safe, mothers should avoid breastfeeding their infants; however, exclusive breastfeeding is recommended during the first months of life if this is not the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> If exclusive breastfeeding is carried out, the provision of extended antiretroviral prophylaxis to the infant decreases the risk of transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> became the first country in the world to eradicate mother-to-child transmission of HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccination">Vaccination</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIV_vaccine" title="HIV vaccine">HIV vaccine</a></div>
<p>Currently there is no licensed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIV_vaccine" title="HIV vaccine">vaccine for HIV or AIDS</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2012Vac_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-UN2012Vac-6">[6]</a></sup> The most effective vaccine trial to date, <a href="/wiki/RV_144" title="RV 144">RV 144</a>, was published in 2009; it found a partial reduction in the risk of transmission of roughly 30%, stimulating some hope in the research community of developing a truly effective vaccine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Further trials of the RV 144 vaccine are ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Management_of_HIV/AIDS" title="Management of HIV/AIDS">Management of HIV/AIDS</a></div>
<p>There is currently no cure, nor an effective HIV vaccine. Treatment consists of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highly_active_antiretroviral_therapy" title="Highly active antiretroviral therapy">highly active antiretroviral therapy</a> (HAART), which slows progression of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LE2011_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-LE2011-155">[155]</a></sup> As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HIV/AIDS&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, more than 6.6 million people were receiving HAART in low- and middle-income countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2011Ten_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN2011Ten-156">[156]</a></sup> Treatment also includes preventive and active treatment of opportunistic infections. As of July 2022<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HIV/AIDS&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, four people have been successfully cleared of HIV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-two_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-two-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Rapid initiation of antiretroviral therapy within one week of diagnosis appear to improve treatment outcomes in low and medium-income settings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiviral_therapy">Antiviral therapy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stribild_bottle_Dutch_labeling.jpg"><img alt="A white prescription bottle with the label Stribild. Next to it are ten green oblong pills with the marking 1 on one side and GSI on the other." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2129" data-file-width="2299" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Stribild_bottle_Dutch_labeling.jpg/220px-Stribild_bottle_Dutch_labeling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Stribild_bottle_Dutch_labeling.jpg/330px-Stribild_bottle_Dutch_labeling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Stribild_bottle_Dutch_labeling.jpg/440px-Stribild_bottle_Dutch_labeling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stribild_bottle_Dutch_labeling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stribild" title="Stribild">Stribild</a></i> – a common once-daily ART regime consisting of <a href="/wiki/Elvitegravir" title="Elvitegravir">elvitegravir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emtricitabine" title="Emtricitabine">emtricitabine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenofovir" title="Tenofovir">tenofovir</a> and the booster <a href="/wiki/Cobicistat" title="Cobicistat">cobicistat</a></div></div></div>
<p>Current HAART options are combinations (or "cocktails") consisting of at least three medications belonging to at least two types, or "classes", of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiretroviral" title="Antiretroviral">antiretroviral</a> agents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOTx2010Pg19_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOTx2010Pg19-161">[161]</a></sup> Initially, treatment is typically a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-nucleoside_reverse_transcriptase_inhibitor" title="Non-nucleoside reverse transcriptase inhibitor">non-nucleoside reverse transcriptase inhibitor</a> (NNRTI) plus two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleoside_analogue_reverse_transcriptase_inhibitor" title="Nucleoside analogue reverse transcriptase inhibitor">nucleoside analog reverse transcriptase inhibitors</a> (NRTIs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-162">[162]</a></sup> Typical NRTIs include: <a href="/wiki/Zidovudine" title="Zidovudine">zidovudine</a> (AZT) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenofovir" title="Tenofovir">tenofovir</a> (TDF) and <a href="/wiki/Lamivudine" title="Lamivudine">lamivudine</a> (3TC) or <a href="/wiki/Emtricitabine" title="Emtricitabine">emtricitabine</a> (FTC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-162">[162]</a></sup> As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HIV/AIDS&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Dolutegravir/lamivudine/tenofovir" title="Dolutegravir/lamivudine/tenofovir">dolutegravir/lamivudine/tenofovir</a> is listed by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> as the first-line treatment for adults, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenofovir/lamivudine/efavirenz" title="Tenofovir/lamivudine/efavirenz">tenofovir/lamivudine/efavirenz</a> as an alternative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2019Up_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2019Up-163">[163]</a></sup> Combinations of agents that include <a href="/wiki/Protease_inhibitor_(pharmacology)" title="Protease inhibitor (pharmacology)">protease inhibitors</a> (PI) are used if the above regimen loses effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOTx2010Pg19_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHOTx2010Pg19-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>The World Health Organization and the United States recommend antiretrovirals in people of all ages (including pregnant women) as soon as the diagnosis is made, regardless of CD4 count.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015Tx_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2015Tx-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAS2014_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAS2014-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DHHS2013_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-DHHS2013-165">[165]</a></sup> Once treatment is begun, it is recommended that it is continued without breaks or "holidays".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup> Many people are diagnosed only after treatment ideally should have begun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup> The desired outcome of treatment is a long-term plasma HIV-RNA count below 50 copies/mL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-6"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup> Levels to determine if treatment is effective are initially recommended after four weeks and once levels fall below 50 copies/mL checks every three to six months are typically adequate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-7"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup> Inadequate control is deemed to be greater than 400 copies/mL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-8"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup> Based on these criteria treatment is effective in more than 95% of people during the first year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-9"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Benefits of treatment include a decreased risk of progression to AIDS and a decreased risk of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> In the developing world, treatment also improves physical and mental health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> With treatment, there is a 70% reduced risk of acquiring tuberculosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOTx2010Pg19_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHOTx2010Pg19-161">[161]</a></sup> Additional benefits include a decreased risk of transmission of the disease to sexual partners and a decrease in mother-to-child transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOTx2010Pg19_161-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHOTx2010Pg19-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> The effectiveness of treatment depends to a large part on compliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-10"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup> Reasons for non-adherence to treatment include poor access to medical care,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> inadequate social supports, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_illness" title="Mental illness">mental illness</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_abuse" title="Drug abuse">drug abuse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> The complexity of treatment regimens (due to pill numbers and dosing frequency) and <a href="/wiki/Adverse_effect" title="Adverse effect">adverse effects</a> may reduce adherence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21406048_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21406048-171">[171]</a></sup> Even though cost is an important issue with some medications,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> 47% of those who needed them were taking them in low- and middle-income countries as of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=HIV/AIDS&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2011Ten_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN2011Ten-156">[156]</a></sup> and the rate of adherence is similar in low-income and high-income countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>Specific adverse events are related to the antiretroviral agent taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montessori2004_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montessori2004-174">[174]</a></sup> Some relatively common adverse events include: <a href="/wiki/HIV-associated_lipodystrophy" title="HIV-associated lipodystrophy">lipodystrophy syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dyslipidemia" title="Dyslipidemia">dyslipidemia</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes mellitus</a>, especially with protease inhibitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M121_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-M121-27">[27]</a></sup> Other common symptoms include diarrhea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montessori2004_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-Montessori2004-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgoyne2008_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burgoyne2008-175">[175]</a></sup> and an increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Newer recommended treatments are associated with fewer adverse effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-11"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup> Certain medications may be associated with <a href="/wiki/Birth_defect" title="Birth defect">birth defects</a> and therefore may be unsuitable for women hoping to have children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-12"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Treatment recommendations for children are somewhat different from those for adults. The World Health Organization recommends treating all children less than five years of age; children above five are treated like adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOCARV2013_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOCARV2013-177">[177]</a></sup> The United States guidelines recommend treating all children less than 12 months of age and all those with HIV RNA counts greater than 100,000 copies/mL between one year and five years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DHHS2014_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-DHHS2014-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Medicines_Agency" title="European Medicines Agency">European Medicines Agency</a> (EMA) has recommended the granting of marketing authorizations for two new antiretroviral (ARV) medicines, <a href="/wiki/Rilpivirine" title="Rilpivirine">rilpivirine</a> (Rekambys) and <a href="/wiki/Cabotegravir" title="Cabotegravir">cabotegravir</a> (Vocabria), to be used together for the treatment of people with human immunodeficiency virus type 1 (HIV-1) infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMA_PR_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-EMA_PR-179">[179]</a></sup> The two medicines are the first ARVs that come in a long-acting injectable formulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMA_PR_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-EMA_PR-179">[179]</a></sup> This means that instead of daily pills, people receive intramuscular injections monthly or every two months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMA_PR_179-2"><a href="#cite_note-EMA_PR-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>The combination of Rekambys and Vocabria injection is intended for maintenance treatment of adults who have undetectable HIV levels in the blood (viral load less than 50 copies/ml) with their current ARV treatment, and when the virus has not developed resistance to a certain class of anti-HIV medicines called non-nucleoside reverse transcriptase inhibitors (NNRTIs) and integrase strand transfer inhibitors (INIs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMA_PR_179-3"><a href="#cite_note-EMA_PR-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cabotegravir/rilpivirine" title="Cabotegravir/rilpivirine">Cabotegravir combined with rilpivirine</a> (Cabenuva) is a complete regimen for the treatment of human immunodeficiency virus type 1 (HIV-1) infection in adults to replace a current antiretroviral regimen in those who are virologically suppressed on a stable antiretroviral regimen with no history of treatment failure and with no known or suspected resistance to either <a href="/wiki/Cabotegravir" title="Cabotegravir">cabotegravir</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rilpivirine" title="Rilpivirine">rilpivirine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDA_PR_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDA_PR-180">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opportunistic_infections">Opportunistic infections</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Opportunistic_infection#Opportunistic_Infection_and_HIV/AIDS" title="Opportunistic infection">Opportunistic infection § Opportunistic Infection and HIV/AIDS</a></div>
<p>Measures to prevent opportunistic infections are effective in many people with HIV/AIDS. In addition to improving current disease, treatment with antiretrovirals reduces the risk of developing additional opportunistic infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montessori2004_174-2"><a href="#cite_note-Montessori2004-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Adults and adolescents who are living with HIV (even on anti-retroviral therapy) with no evidence of active tuberculosis in settings with high tuberculosis burden should receive <a href="/wiki/Isoniazid" title="Isoniazid">isoniazid preventive therapy</a> (IPT); the <a href="/wiki/Mantoux_test" title="Mantoux test">tuberculin skin test</a> can be used to help decide if IPT is needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOHIVTB2011_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOHIVTB2011-182">[182]</a></sup> Children with HIV may benefit from screening for tuberculosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">Vaccination</a> against <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis" title="Hepatitis">hepatitis</a> A and B is advised for all people at risk of HIV before they become infected; however, it may also be given after infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laurence_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laurence-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole" title="Trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole">Trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole</a> prophylaxis between four and six weeks of age, and ceasing breastfeeding of infants born to HIV-positive mothers, is recommended in resource-limited settings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2011ONESIXTY_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN2011ONESIXTY-185">[185]</a></sup> It is also recommended to prevent PCP when a person's CD4 count is below 200 cells/uL and in those who have or have previously had PCP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PCP2011_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-PCP2011-186">[186]</a></sup> People with substantial immunosuppression are also advised to receive prophylactic therapy for <a href="/wiki/Toxoplasmosis" title="Toxoplasmosis">toxoplasmosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_avium-intracellulare_infection" title="Mycobacterium avium-intracellulare infection">MAC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PEPpocketguide_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-PEPpocketguide-187">[187]</a></sup> Appropriate preventive measures reduced the rate of these infections by 50% between 1992 and 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InfectionBook2008_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-InfectionBook2008-188">[188]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Influenza_vaccination" title="Influenza vaccination">Influenza vaccination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pneumococcal_polysaccharide_vaccine" title="Pneumococcal polysaccharide vaccine">pneumococcal polysaccharide vaccine</a> are often recommended in people with HIV/AIDS with some evidence of benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutrition_and_HIV/AIDS" title="Nutrition and HIV/AIDS">Nutrition and HIV/AIDS</a></div>
<p>The World Health Organization (WHO) has issued recommendations regarding nutrient requirements in HIV/AIDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_nutrients_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_nutrients-191">[191]</a></sup> A generally healthy diet is promoted. Dietary intake of micronutrients at <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake" title="Reference Daily Intake">RDA</a> levels by HIV-infected adults is recommended by the WHO; higher intake of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, and iron can produce adverse effects in HIV-positive adults, and is not recommended unless there is documented deficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_nutrients_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_nutrients-191">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Dietary supplementation for people who are infected with HIV and who have inadequate nutrition or dietary deficiencies may strengthen their immune systems or help them recover from infections; however, evidence indicating an overall benefit in morbidity or reduction in mortality is not consistent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence for supplementation with <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a> is mixed with some tentative evidence of benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>  For pregnant and lactating women with HIV, <a href="/wiki/Multivitamin" title="Multivitamin">multivitamin</a> supplement improves outcomes for both mothers and children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siegfried2012_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siegfried2012-197">[197]</a></sup>  If the pregnant or lactating mother has been advised to take anti-retroviral medication to prevent mother-to-child HIV transmission, multivitamin supplements should not replace these treatments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siegfried2012_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-Siegfried2012-197">[197]</a></sup> There is some evidence that vitamin A supplementation in children with an HIV infection reduces mortality and improves growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_medicine">Alternative medicine</span></h3>
<p>In the US, approximately 60% of people with HIV use various forms of <a href="/wiki/Alternative_medicine" title="Alternative medicine">complementary or alternative medicine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18608078_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18608078-199">[199]</a></sup> whose effectiveness has not been established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15969772_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15969772-200">[200]</a></sup>  There is not enough evidence to support the use of <a href="/wiki/Herbal_medicine" title="Herbal medicine">herbal medicines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> There is insufficient evidence to recommend or support the use of <a href="/wiki/Medical_cannabis" title="Medical cannabis">medical cannabis</a> to try to increase appetite or weight gain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lutge_2013_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-lutge_2013-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HIV-AIDS_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/HIV-AIDS_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/290px-HIV-AIDS_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/HIV-AIDS_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/435px-HIV-AIDS_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/HIV-AIDS_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/580px-HIV-AIDS_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HIV-AIDS_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Deaths due to HIV/AIDS per million people in 2012 <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1054258005">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul li{list-style:none}@media(max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{padding-left:1.6em;text-indent:-1.6em}}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="refbegin refbegin-columns references-column-count references-column-count-3" style="column-count: 3;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffff20; color:black;"> </span> 0</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffe820; color:black;"> </span> 1–4</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffd820; color:black;"> </span> 5–12</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffc020; color:black;"> </span> 13–34</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffa020; color:black;"> </span> 35–61</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a20; color:black;"> </span> 62–134</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f08015; color:black;"> </span> 135–215</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#e06815; color:black;"> </span> 216–458</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d85010; color:black;"> </span> 459–1,402</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d02010; color:white;"> </span> 1,403–5,828</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>HIV/AIDS has become a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronic_(medicine)" title="Chronic (medicine)">chronic</a> rather than an acutely fatal disease in many areas of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knoll2007_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knoll2007-203">[203]</a></sup> Prognosis varies between people, and both the CD4 count and viral load are useful for predicted outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M118_30-14"><a href="#cite_note-M118-30">[30]</a></sup> Without treatment, average survival time after infection with HIV is estimated to be 9 to 11 years, depending on the HIV subtype.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNAIDS2007_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-UNAIDS2007-7">[7]</a></sup> After the diagnosis of AIDS, if treatment is not available, survival ranges between 6 and 19 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan2_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan2-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HAART" title="HAART">HAART</a> and appropriate prevention of opportunistic infections reduces the death rate by 80%, and raises the life expectancy for a newly diagnosed young adult to 20–50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knoll2007_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-Knoll2007-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LifeExpecr2008_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-LifeExpecr2008-206">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schack2006_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schack2006-207">[207]</a></sup> This is between two thirds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LifeExpecr2008_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-LifeExpecr2008-206">[206]</a></sup> and nearly that of the general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-13"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> If treatment is started late in the infection, prognosis is not as good:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-14"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup> for example, if treatment is begun following the diagnosis of AIDS, life expectancy is ~10–40 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deut2010_31-15"><a href="#cite_note-Deut2010-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knoll2007_203-2"><a href="#cite_note-Knoll2007-203">[203]</a></sup> Half of infants born with HIV die before two years of age without treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN2011ONESIXTY_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN2011ONESIXTY-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HIV-AIDS_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg"><img alt="A map of the world where much of it is colored yellow or orange except for sub Saharan Africa which is colored red or dark red" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/HIV-AIDS_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/290px-HIV-AIDS_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/HIV-AIDS_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/435px-HIV-AIDS_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/HIV-AIDS_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/580px-HIV-AIDS_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HIV-AIDS_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Disability-adjusted_life_year" title="Disability-adjusted life year">Disability-adjusted life year</a> for HIV and AIDS per 100,000 inhabitants as of 2004. <div> <table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;"> <tbody><tr> <td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;"> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#b3b3b3; color:black;"> </span> <small>no data</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffff65;
